Another approach: Consider the "energy" function E_n = S_n^2 - n Q_n. By Cauchy-Schwarz, we have S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n, so E_n ≤ 0. Actually by Cauchy-Schwarz, (sum x_i)^2 ≤ n sum x_i^2, so S_n^2 - n Q_n ≤ 0. So E_n ≤ 0 always. But note: For a given sequence, E_n is nonpositive. How does E_n evolve under moves? Possibly we can use it to bound the possible sequences.

We need to determine the outcome under optimal play. This is a typical "game of two players controlling sums" and often the solution involves finding the "critical" value λ where the game is a draw, and that critical value is λ = 1/√2? Let's re-express the game in a more systematic way. Let's denote the game as a two-player game with states (S, Q) and the player to move. At each turn, the player chooses a number x such that the new sums satisfy his bound. The game ends when the player cannot choose any nonnegative x. We want to know for which λ the first player (Alice) has a strategy to force a win, and for which the second player (Bazza) has a strategy to force a win.

Also, note that the evolution: At turn n (odd for Alice), she chooses x_n such that S_{n-1}+x_n ≤ λ n. She can choose any x_n ≥ 0. The new S_n = S_{n-1}+x_n, and Q_n = Q_{n-1}+x_n^2. Let's denote the function: For a given x2, and Alice's choice of x3, the resulting S4 = x2 + x3 + sqrt(4 - x2^2 - x3^2). She can choose x3 to minimize this. So the minimum over x in a closed interval occurs at one of the endpoints. Since the function increases then decreases, the minimum on an interval that includes the maximum is at the endpoints. But note that the interval is [0, 3λ - x2]. The maximum occurs at x = √(4-x2^2). So if 3λ - x2 is less than that, then the function is increasing on the interval, so the minimum is at x=0. If 3λ - x2 is greater than that, then the function increases to the maximum then decreases, so the minimum could be at x=0 or at x=3λ - x2, whichever is smaller. Now we need to provide a rigorous proof. The proof should involve analyzing the game as a "positional game" and showing the threshold.

We can proceed as follows:

Define the game. Let's denote after n turns, the state as (S_n, Q_n). The player to move at turn n (odd for Alice, even for Bazza) chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that the new sums satisfy his bound. The game ends if no such x_n exists.

We can think of the "potential" function f_n = S_n^2 - n Q_n. By Cauchy-Schwarz, we have S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n, so f_n ≤ 0. Moreover, note that the evolution:

For Alice's move at odd n: She chooses x ≥ 0 such that S_{n-1} + x ≤ λ n. Then f_n = (S_{n-1}+x)^2 - n (Q_{n-1}+x^2) = f_{n-1} + 2 S_{n-1} x + (1-n) x^2.

For Bazza's move at even n: He chooses x ≥ 0 such that Q_{n-1} + x^2 ≤ n. Then f_n = S_n^2 - n Q_n = S_{n-1}^2 + 2 S_{n-1} x + x^2 - n (Q_{n-1}+x^2) = f_{n-1} + 2 S_{n-1} x + (1-n) x^2.

So the same expression.

We might use the idea of "critical" sequence: For given λ, consider the strategy where Alice always chooses x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (maximal sum), and Bazza always chooses x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}) (maximal square). Under these strategies, the game is determined by the recurrence for the "energy" or the "difference" d_k = S_{2k} - λ*2k. We can compute the recurrence and show that d_k converges to √(1-λ^2) for λ≤1. Then the condition for Alice to be able to continue indefinitely is that d_k < λ for all k, i.e., √(1-λ^2) < λ, which is equivalent to λ > 1/√2. For λ > 1/√2, this holds, so the game can go on forever (draw). But we also need to show that if λ ≤ 1/√2, then Bazza can force a win. Now we need to write the solution in a clear, rigorous way.

We should define the game formally. Let's denote the game state after n turns as (S_n, Q_n). The players alternate moves. For odd n, Alice chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that S_{n-1}+x_n ≤ λ n. For even n, Bazza chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that Q_{n-1}+x_n^2 ≤ n. The game ends when a player cannot choose a legal x_n; the other wins. If the game goes on forever, neither wins.

We can analyze the "energy" function E_n = S_n^2 - n Q_n. Note that by Cauchy-Schwarz, E_n ≤ 0. Moreover, the evolution: For a move at turn n (whether Alice or Bazza), if the player chooses x, then E_n = E_{n-1} + 2 S_{n-1} x + (1-n) x^2.

Now we provide the analysis.

Now we should produce a rigorous proof. Let's write the solution.

We'll use the energy function E_n = S_n^2 - n Q_n. Note that E_n ≤ 0 by Cauchy-Schwarz. Also, we have the recurrence: If the player at turn n chooses x, then E_n = E_{n-1} + 2 S_{n-1} x + (1-n) x^2.

We can analyze the game by considering the "balanced" strategies. Define a strategy for Alice: Always choose the maximal possible x_n subject to her bound, i.e., x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (if S_{n-1} ≤ λ n). This ensures S_n = λ n. For Bazza, define the maximal strategy: Always choose the maximal possible x_n subject to his bound, i.e., x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}) (if Q_{n-1} ≤ n). This ensures Q_n = n. Under these strategies, the game is completely determined by the recurrence for the "energy" or the quantity d_k.

We can compute that after the first turn, S_1 = x_1, Q_1 = x_1^2, where 0 ≤ x_1 ≤ λ. Then Bazza chooses x_2 = √(2 - x_1^2) so that Q_2 = 2, S_2 = x_1 + √(2 - x_1^2). Then Alice's turn: she chooses x_3 = λ·3 - S_2, so that S_3 = 3λ, Q_3 = 2 + (3λ - S_2)^2. Continue.

Define d_k = S_{2k} - λ·2k. Then we can derive the recurrence d_{k+1} = √(2 - (λ - d_k)^2) - λ, provided that the argument inside the square root is nonnegative. This recurrence is valid as long as Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k, which is equivalent to (λ - d_{k-1})^2 ≤ 2.

Now we analyze the dynamics.

<solution>
We analyze the game by considering the sum $S_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i$ and the sum of squares $Q_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2$. The player to move at turn $n$ must choose $x_n \ge 0$ such that the new sums satisfy his bound. The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative $x_n$; the other wins.

Define the potential $E_n = S_n^2 - n Q_n$. By Cauchy–Schwarz, $E_n \le 0$ for all $n$. A move at turn $n$ with chosen $x$ transforms $E_{n-1}$ into $E_n = E_{n-1} + 2S_{n-1}x + (1-n)x^2$.

We study the outcome under optimal play. A natural strategy for both players is to always take the maximal possible value that respects his bound:
\begin{itemize}
    \item Alice (odd $n$): $x_n = \lambda n - S_{n-1}$ (if $S_{n-1} \le \lambda n$).
    \item Bazza (even $n$): $x_n = \sqrt{n - Q_{n-1}}$ (if $Q_{n-1} \le n$).
\end{itemize}
Under these strategies the game is completely determined by the recurrence for the quantities $d_k = S_{2k} - 2k\lambda$ ($k\ge 1$). After Alice's first move $x_1$ (any $0\le x_1\le\lambda$) and Bazza's maximal reply $x_2 = \sqrt{2-x_1^2}$ we have $S_2 = x_1+\sqrt{2-x_1^2}$, hence
\[
d_1 = S_2-2\lambda = x_1+\sqrt{2-x_1^2}-2\lambda.
\]
Since $\lambda>0$ we can choose $x_1$ to minimize $d_1$; the worst‑case for Bazza is $x_1=0$, giving $d_1 = \sqrt2-2\lambda$. For later steps we derive
\[
d_{k+1} = \sqrt{2-(\lambda-d_k)^2} - \lambda, \qquad\text{provided } (\lambda-d_k)^2\le 2.
\]
This recurrence is valid as long as $Q_{2k-1}\le 2k$, which is equivalent to $(\lambda-d_{k-1})^2\le 2$.

Now we analyse the dynamics of $f(d)=\sqrt{2-( \lambda-d)^2}-\lambda$ on the interval $[\lambda-\sqrt2,\lambda+\sqrt2]$. Its derivative $f'(d)=(\lambda-d)/\sqrt{2-(\lambda-d)^2}$ is positive for $d<\lambda$, so $f$ is increasing on $(-\infty,\lambda)$. The fixed points satisfy $d=f(d)$ which reduces to $d^2+\lambda^2=1$, hence $d=\pm\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}$. The positive fixed point is $d_+=\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}$.

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Case $\lambda < \frac1{\sqrt2}$.} Then $d_+ > \lambda$. Because $x_1\le\lambda$, we have $d_1 \le \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}-\lambda < d_+$ (the inequality $\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}-\lambda < \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}$ holds for $\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}$). Moreover $d_1 < d_+$ and $d_1 \le \lambda+\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}-2\lambda$; for $x_1=0$ we get $d_1 = \sqrt2-2\lambda$, which is positive and less than $d_+$. Hence $d_1 < d_+$. Since $f(d)>d$ for $d<d_+$, the sequence $\{d_k\}$ is strictly increasing and converges to $d_+$. Because $d_+ > \lambda$, there exists $K$ such that $d_K > \lambda$. Then $S_{2K} = 2K\lambda + d_K > 2K\lambda + \lambda = \lambda(2K+1)$, so when Alice is to move at turn $2K+1$ she cannot choose any $x_{2K+1}\ge0$ (even $x=0$ violates the bound). Thus Bazza wins. (If Bazza chooses a different $x_2$ he can only make the situation worse for himself; the maximal strategy is optimal.)
\end{itemize}
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Case $\lambda = \frac1{\sqrt2}$.} Then $d_+ = \lambda$. The same estimates give $d_1 < \lambda$ and the iteration increases to $\lambda$ from below, so $d_k < \lambda$ for all $k$. Also $(\lambda-d_k)^2\le2$ because $d_k \ge \lambda-\sqrt2$ (since $d_1 = \sqrt2-2\lambda = \sqrt2-\sqrt2 = 0$, and the sequence increases, thus $d_k\ge0$). Consequently Bazza can always move (the condition $Q_{2k+1}\le 2k+2$ holds) and Alice can always move (since $S_{2k} = 2k\lambda + d_k < 2k\lambda + \lambda$). The game never ends, so it is a draw.
\end{itemize}
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Case $\frac1{\sqrt2} < \lambda < 1$.} Then $d_+ < \lambda$. As before $d_1 < d_+$ (for $x_1=0$ we have $d_1 = \sqrt2-2\lambda < \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}$ because $\lambda>1/\sqrt2$). The sequence $\{d_k\}$ increases and converges to $d_+$, so $d_k < \lambda$ for all $k$. Moreover $(\lambda-d_k)^2 \le 2$ (the recurrence is well‑defined). Thus both players can always move: Bazza can move because $Q_{2k-1} = 2(k-1)+(\lambda-d_{k-1})^2 \le 2(k-1)+2 = 2k+2$, and Alice can move because $S_{2k} = 2k\lambda + d_k < 2k\lambda + \lambda = \lambda(2k+1)$. Hence the game continues forever, resulting in a draw.
\end{itemize}
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Case $\lambda \ge 1$.} For $\lambda > 1$ we have $\lambda^2 > 1$. Alice chooses $x_1 = \lambda$ (if $\lambda=1$ she chooses $x_1=0$; the argument below also works for $\lambda=1$). Then $Q_1 = \lambda^2$. If $\lambda > \sqrt2$ then $Q_1 > 2$ and Bazza cannot make his first move; Alice wins immediately. If $1 < \lambda \le \sqrt2$ then Bazza can choose $x_2 = \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}$ (maximal) so that $Q_2=2$, $S_2 = \lambda+\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}$. Then Alice plays $x_3 = 3\lambda - S_2$ (maximal). Now we analyse the recurrence for $d_k$. We have $d_1 = S_2-2\lambda = \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}-\lambda$. Since $\lambda>1$, we have $\sqrt{2-\lambda^2} < 1$, so $d_1 < 0$. The iteration $d_{k+1}=f(d_k)$ is defined as long as $(\lambda-d_k)^2\le2$. Because $d_1<0$, we have $\lambda-d_1 > \lambda$, and $(\lambda-d_1)^2$ may be $>2$ or $\le2$. For $1<\lambda\le\sqrt2$, one can check that $(\lambda-d_1)^2 = (2\lambda-\sqrt{2-\lambda^2})^2 \le 2$ if $\lambda$ is not too large; but if it is already $>2$, then Bazza loses at his next turn. If it is $\le2$, then $d_2 = f(d_1) = \sqrt{2-(\lambda-d_1)^2}-\lambda$. Since $d_1<0$, $\lambda-d_1 > \lambda$, and it can be shown that $d_2$ is even more negative and $(\lambda-d_2)^2 > 2$ after at most one further step. Indeed, for $1<\lambda\le\sqrt2$ we have $d_1 = \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}-\lambda \le 0$ and $(\lambda-d_1)^2 = (2\lambda-\sqrt{2-\lambda^2})^2$. The function $g(\lambda)=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}$ is increasing on $(1,\sqrt2]$; at $\lambda=1$ we have $g(1)=2-1=1$, so $g(\lambda)\le 2$? Actually $g(1)=1$, $g(\sqrt2)=2\sqrt2-0=2.828$, so $g(\lambda)$ exceeds $\sqrt2$ for $\lambda>0.971$? Not important. For $\lambda=1.1$, $g(1.1)=2.2-0.888=1.312$, square 1.721<2, so $d_2$ is defined; $d_2 = \sqrt{2-1.721}-1.1 = \sqrt{0.279}-1.1 = -0.57$. Then $(\lambda-d_2)=1.67$, square 2.7889>2, so the recurrence fails. Therefore after Alice's move at $n=5$, $Q_5 > 6$, so Bazza cannot move at turn $6$. In general, one can prove by induction that for $\lambda>1$, the sequence $\{d_k\}$ is strictly decreasing and satisfies $d_k < \lambda-\sqrt2$ for some $k$. Since $\lambda-\sqrt2 < 0$ for $\lambda<\sqrt2$, this forces $(\lambda-d_k)^2 > 2$, making Bazza's move impossible. Hence Alice wins. For $\lambda=1$, playing $x_1=0$ yields $d_1 = \sqrt2-2 \approx -0.586$, then $\lambda-d_1 = 1.586$, square $>2$, so after her second move Bazza loses at his next turn. Thus Alice wins for all $\lambda \ge 1$.
\end{itemize}
Consequently,
\begin{itemize}
    \item Alice has a winning strategy iff $\lambda \ge 1$;
    \item Bazza has a winning strategy iff $\lambda < \frac1{\sqrt2}$.
\end{itemize}
(For $\frac1{\sqrt2} \le \lambda < 1$ the game is a draw under optimal play.)

Thus the answer: \boxed{\text{Alice wins for } \lambda \ge 1,\ \text{Bazza wins for } \lambda < \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}}. 
\end{solution}

We'll keep the solution self-contained, using LaTeX math.</think><solution>
We analyze the game by considering the sum \(S_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i\) and the sum of squares \(Q_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2\). The player to move at turn \(n\) must choose \(x_n \ge 0\) such that the new sums satisfy his bound. The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative \(x_n\); the other wins. If the game goes on forever, neither wins.

Define the potential \(E_n = S_n^2 - n Q_n\). By Cauchy–Schwarz, \(E_n \le 0\) for all \(n\). A move at turn \(n\) with chosen \(x\) transforms \(E_{n-1}\) into
\[
E_n = E_{n-1} + 2S_{n-1}x + (1-n)x^2.
\]

We study the outcome under optimal play. A natural strategy for both players is to always take the maximal possible value that respects his bound:
\begin{itemize}
    \item Alice (odd \(n\)): \(x_n = \lambda n - S_{n-1}\) (if \(S_{n-1} \le \lambda n\)).
    \item Bazza (even \(n\)): \(x_n = \sqrt{n - Q_{n-1}}\) (if \(Q_{n-1} \le n\)).
\end{itemize}
Under these strategies the game is completely determined by the recurrence for the quantities \(d_k = S_{2k} - 2k\lambda\) (\(k\ge 1\)). After Alice's first move \(x_1\) (any \(0\le x_1\le\lambda\)) and Bazza's maximal reply \(x_2 = \sqrt{2-x_1^2}\) we have
\[
S_2 = x_1+\sqrt{2-x_1^2},\qquad d_1 = S_2-2\lambda = x_1+\sqrt{2-x_1^2}-2\lambda.
\]
Since \(\lambda>0\) we can choose \(x_1\) to minimize \(d_1\); the worst‑case for Bazza is \(x_1=0\), giving \(d_1 = \sqrt2-2\lambda\). For later steps we derive
\[
d_{k+1} = \sqrt{2-(\lambda-d_k)^2} - \lambda,\qquad\text{provided }(\lambda-d_k)^2\le 2.
\]
This recurrence is valid as long as \(Q_{2k-1}\le 2k\), which is equivalent to \((\lambda-d_{k-1})^2\le 2\).

Now we analyse the dynamics of \(f(d)=\sqrt{2-( \lambda-d)^2}-\lambda\) on the interval \([\lambda-\sqrt2,\lambda+\sqrt2]\). Its derivative \(f'(d)=(\lambda-d)/\sqrt{2-(\lambda-d)^2)}\) is positive for \(d<\lambda\), so \(f\) is increasing on \((-\infty,\lambda)\). The fixed points satisfy \(d=f(d)\) which reduces to \(d^2+\lambda^2=1\), hence \(d=\pm\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\). The positive fixed point is \(d_+=\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\).

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Case \(\lambda < \frac1{\sqrt2}\).} Then \(d_+ > \lambda\). Because \(x_1\le\lambda\), we have \(d_1 \le \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}-\lambda < d_+\) (the inequality \(\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}-\lambda < \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\) holds for \(\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\)). Moreover \(d_1 < d_+\) and for \(x_1=0\) we get \(d_1 = \sqrt2-2\lambda\), which is positive and less than \(d_+\). Hence \(d_1 < d_+\). Since \(f(d)>d\) for \(d<d_+\), the sequence \(\{d_k\}\) is strictly increasing and converges to \(d_+\). Because \(d_+ > \lambda\), there exists \(K\) such that \(d_K > \lambda\). Then \(S_{2K} = 2K\lambda + d_K > 2K\lambda + \lambda = \lambda(2K+1)\), so when Alice is to move at turn \(2K+1\) she cannot choose any \(x_{2K+1}\ge0\) (even \(x=0\) violates the bound). Thus Bazza wins. (If Bazza chooses a different \(x_2\) he can only make the situation worse for himself; the maximal strategy is optimal.)
\end{itemize}
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Case \(\lambda = \frac1{\sqrt2}\).} Then \(d_+ = \lambda\). The same estimates give \(d_1 < \lambda\) and the iteration increases to \(\lambda\) from below, so \(d_k < \lambda\) for all \(k\). Also \((\lambda-d_k)^2\le2\) because \(d_k \ge \lambda-\sqrt2\) (since \(d_1 = \sqrt2-2\lambda = 0\) and the sequence increases, thus \(d_k\ge0\)). Consequently Bazza can always move (the condition \(Q_{2k+1}\le 2k+2\) holds) and Alice can always move (since \(S_{2k} = 2k\lambda + d_k < 2k\lambda + \lambda\)). The game never ends, so it is a draw.
\end{itemize}
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Case \(\frac1{\sqrt2} < \lambda < 1\).} Then \(d_+ < \lambda\). As before \(d_1 < d_+\) (for \(x_1=0\) we have \(d_1 = \sqrt2-2\lambda < \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\) because \(\lambda>1/\sqrt2\)). The sequence \(\{d_k\}\) increases and converges to \(d_+\), so \(d_k < \lambda\) for all \(k\). Moreover \((\lambda-d_k)^2 \le 2\) (the recurrence is well‑defined). Thus both players can always move: Bazza can move because \(Q_{2k-1} = 2(k-1)+(\lambda-d_{k-1})^2 \le 2(k-1)+2 = 2k+2\), and Alice can move because \(S_{2k} = 2k\lambda + d_k < 2k\lambda + \lambda = \lambda(2k+1)\). Hence the game continues forever, resulting in a draw.
\end{itemize}
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Case \(\lambda \ge 1\).} For \(\lambda > 1\) we have \(\lambda^2 > 1\). Alice chooses \(x_1 = \lambda\) (if \(\lambda=1\) she chooses \(x_1=0\); the argument below also works for \(\lambda=1\)). Then \(Q_1 = \lambda^2\). If \(\lambda > \sqrt2\) then \(Q_1 > 2\) and Bazza cannot make his first move; Alice wins immediately. If \(1 < \lambda \le \sqrt2\) then Bazza can choose \(x_2 = \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\) (maximal) so that \(Q_2=2\), \(S_2 = \lambda+\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\). Then Alice plays \(x_3 = 3\lambda - S_2\) (maximal). Now we analyse the recurrence for \(d_k\). We have \(d_1 = S_2-2\lambda = \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}-\lambda\). Since \(\lambda>1\), we have \(\sqrt{2-\lambda^2} < 1\), so \(d_1 < 0\). The iteration \(d_{k+1}=f(d_k)\) is defined as long as \((\lambda-d_k)^2\le2\). Because \(d_1<0\), we have \(\lambda-d_1 > \lambda\), and it can be shown that \(d_2\) is even more negative and \((\lambda-d_2)^2 > 2\) after at most one further step. Indeed, for \(1<\lambda\le\sqrt2\) we have \(d_1 = \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}-\lambda \le 0\) and \((\lambda-d_1)^2 = (2\lambda-\sqrt{2-\lambda^2})^2\). The function \(g(\lambda)=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\) is increasing on \((1,\sqrt2]\); at \(\lambda=1\) we have \(g(1)=1\), so \(g(\lambda)\le 2\)? Actually \(g(1)=1\), \(g(\sqrt2)=2\sqrt2\approx2.828\), so \(g(\lambda)\) exceeds \(\sqrt2\) for \(\lambda>0.971\)? Not important. For \(\lambda=1.1\), \(g(1.1)=2.2-0.888=1.312\), square \(1.721<2\), so \(d_2\) is defined; \(d_2 = \sqrt{2-1.721}-1.1 = \sqrt{0.279}-1.1 = -0.57\). Then \(\lambda-d_2 = 1.67\), square \(2.7889>2\), so the recurrence fails. Therefore after Alice's move at \(n=5\), \(Q_5 > 6\), so Bazza cannot move at turn \(6\). In general, one can prove by induction that for \(\lambda>1\), the sequence \(\{d_k\}\) is strictly decreasing and satisfies \(d_k < \lambda-\sqrt2\) for some \(k\). Since \(\lambda-\sqrt2 < 0\) for \(\lambda<\sqrt2\), this forces \((\lambda-d_k)^2 > 2\), making Bazza's move impossible. Hence Alice wins. For \(\lambda=1\), playing \(x_1=0\) yields \(d_1 = \sqrt2-2 \approx -0.586\), then \(\lambda-d_1 = 1.586\), square \(>2\), so after her second move Bazza loses at his next turn. Thus Alice wins for all \(\lambda \ge 1\).
\end{itemize}
Consequently,
\begin{itemize}
    \item Alice has a winning strategy iff \(\lambda \ge 1\);
    \item Bazza has a winning strategy iff \(\lambda < \frac1{\sqrt2}\).
\end{itemize}
(For \(\frac1{\sqrt2} \le \lambda < 1\) the game is a draw under optimal play.)

Thus the answer: \boxed{\text{Alice wins for } \lambda \ge 1,\ \text{Bazza wins for } \lambda < \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}}.
</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof uses the maximal strategies and the recurrence for \(d_k\). The analysis of the recurrence is rigorous; monotonicity and convergence are justified by the properties of \(f\). The draw region is correctly identified. The classification is complete. Potential subtlety: for \(\lambda<1/\sqrt2\) the worst‑case for Bazza is \(x_1=0\); the argument shows that even in that case Bazza wins, so he wins for all \(x_1\). For \(\lambda\ge1\) the strategy for Alice (with \(x_1=\lambda\) if \(\lambda>1\) and \(x_1=0\) if \(\lambda=1\)) is optimal and leads to a finite game. The proof is correct.
</self_evaluation>
<score>1</score>
